In 1945 Truman told Eisenhower during the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a> that if desired, the president would help the general win the 1948 election,<sup id="cite_ref-pusey1956_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pusey1956-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> and in 1947 he offered to run as Eisenhower's running mate on the Democratic ticket if MacArthur won the Republican nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt20030711_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20030711-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the election approached, other prominent citizens and politicians from both parties urged Eisenhower to run for president. In January 1948, after learning of plans in New Hampshire to elect delegates supporting him for the forthcoming <a href="/wiki/1948_Republican_National_Convention" title="1948 Republican National Convention">Republican National Convention</a>, Eisenhower stated through the Army that he was "not available for and could not accept nomination to high political office"; "life-long professional soldiers", he wrote, "in the absence of some obvious and overriding reason, [should] abstain from seeking high political office".<sup id="cite_ref-pusey1956_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pusey1956-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower maintained no political party affiliation during this time. Many believed he was forgoing his only opportunity to be president; Republican <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Dewey" title="Thomas E. Dewey">Thomas E. Dewey</a> was considered the other probable winner, would presumably serve two terms, and Eisenhower, at age 66 in 1956, would then be too old.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President_at_Columbia_University_and_NATO_Supreme_Commander">President at Columbia University and NATO Supreme Commander</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: President at Columbia University and NATO Supreme Commander">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018,_Berlin,_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="564" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018,_Berlin,_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Supreme Commanders of the <a href="/wiki/Allied_Control_Council" title="Allied Control Council">Four Powers</a> on June 5, 1945, in Berlin: <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Montgomery" title="Bernard Montgomery" class="mw-redirect">Bernard Montgomery</a>, Dwight D. Eisenhower, <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Zhukov" title="Georgy Zhukov">Georgy Zhukov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Lattre_de_Tassigny" title="Jean de Lattre de Tassigny">Jean de Lattre de Tassigny</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1948, Eisenhower became President of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> university in New York. The assignment was described as not being a good fit in either direction.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> During that year Eisenhower's memoir, <i><a href="/wiki/Crusade_in_Europe" title="Crusade in Europe">Crusade in Europe</a></i>, was published.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Critics regarded it as one of the finest U.S. military memoirs, and it was a major financial success as well. Eisenhower's profit on the book was substantially aided by an unprecedented ruling by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_the_Treasury" title="U.S. Department of the Treasury" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Department of the Treasury</a> that Eisenhower was not a professional writer, but rather, marketing the lifetime asset of his experiences, and thus he only had to pay capital gains tax on his $635,000 advance instead of the much higher personal tax rate. This ruling saved Eisenhower about $400,000.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower's stint as the president of Columbia University was punctuated by his activity within the <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="Council on Foreign Relations">Council on Foreign Relations</a>, a study group he led as president concerning the political and military implications of the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_American_Assembly" title="The American Assembly">The American Assembly</a>, Eisenhower's "vision of a great cultural center where business, professional and governmental leaders could meet from time to time to discuss and reach conclusions concerning problems of a social and political nature". His biographer <a href="/wiki/Blanche_Wiesen_Cook" title="Blanche Wiesen Cook">Blanche Wiesen Cook</a> suggested that this period served as "the political education of General Eisenhower", since he had to prioritize wide-ranging educational, administrative, and financial demands for the university. Through his involvement in the Council on Foreign Relations, he also gained exposure to economic analysis, which would become the bedrock of his understanding in economic policy. "Whatever General Eisenhower knows about economics, he has learned at the study group meetings," one Aid to Europe member claimed.</p>
<p>Eisenhower accepted the presidency of the university to expand his ability to promote "the American form of democracy" through education. He was clear on this point to the trustees involved in the search committee. He informed them that his main purpose was "to promote the basic concepts of education in a democracy." As a result, he was "almost incessantly" devoted to the idea of the American Assembly, a concept he developed into an institution by the end of 1950.</p>
<p>Within months of beginning his tenure as the president of the university, Eisenhower was requested to advise <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_Defense" title="U.S. Secretary of Defense" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Secretary of Defense</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Forrestal" title="James Forrestal">James Forrestal</a> on the unification of the armed services. About six months after his appointment, he became the informal <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> in Washington. Two months later he fell ill, and he spent over a month in recovery at the <a href="/wiki/Augusta_National_Golf_Club" title="Augusta National Golf Club">Augusta National Golf Club</a>. He returned to his post in <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York</a> in mid-May, and in July 1949 took a two-month vacation out-of-state. Because the American Assembly had begun to take shape, he traveled around the country during mid-to-late 1950, building financial support from Columbia Associates, an alumni association.</p>
<p>Eisenhower was unknowingly building resentment and a reputation among the Columbia University faculty and staff as an absentee president who was using the university for his own interests. As a career military man, he naturally had little in common with the academics.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The contacts gained through university and American Assembly fund-raising activities would later become important supporters in Eisenhower's bid for the Republican party nomination and the presidency. Meanwhile, Columbia University's liberal faculty members became disenchanted with the university president's ties to oilmen and businessmen, including Leonard McCollum, the president of <a href="/wiki/Continental_Oil" title="Continental Oil" class="mw-redirect">Continental Oil</a>; Frank Abrams, the chairman of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil_of_New_Jersey" title="Standard Oil of New Jersey" class="mw-redirect">Standard Oil of New Jersey</a>; Bob Kleberg, the president of the King Ranch; H. J. Porter, a Texas oil executive; Bob Woodruff, the president of the <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Corporation" title="Coca-Cola Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Coca-Cola Corporation</a>; and Clarence Francis, the chairman of <a href="/wiki/General_Foods" title="General Foods">General Foods</a>.</p>
<p>As the president of Columbia, Eisenhower gave voice and form to his opinions about the supremacy and difficulties of American democracy. His tenure marked his transformation from military to civilian leadership. His biographer Travis Beal Jacobs also suggested that the alienation of the Columbia faculty contributed to sharp intellectual criticism of him for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The trustees of Columbia University refused to accept Eisenhower's resignation in December 1950, when he took an extended leave from the university to become the Supreme Commander of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization" class="mw-redirect">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO), and he was given operational command of NATO forces in Europe. Eisenhower retired from active service as an Army general on May 31, 1952, and he resumed his presidency of Columbia. He held this position until January 20, 1953, when he became the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>.</p>
<p>NATO did not have strong bipartisan support in Congress at the time that Eisenhower assumed its military command. Eisenhower advised the participating European nations that it would be incumbent upon them to demonstrate their own commitment of troops and equipment to the NATO force before such would come from the war-weary United States.</p>
<p>At home, Eisenhower was more effective in making the case for NATO in Congress than the Truman administration was. By the middle of 1951, American and European support for NATO was substantial enough to give it a genuine military power. Nevertheless, Eisenhower thought that NATO would become a truly European alliance, with the American and Canadian commitments ending after about ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_campaign_of_1952">Presidential campaign of 1952</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Presidential campaign of 1952">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1952" title="United States presidential election, 1952">United States presidential election, 1952</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:I_Like_Ike_button,_1952.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png/220px-I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png/330px-I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="412" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:I_Like_Ike_button,_1952.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"I Like Ike" button from the 1952 campaign</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>President Truman, symbolizing a broad-based desire for an Eisenhower candidacy for president, again in 1951 pressed him to run for the office as a Democrat. It was at this time that Eisenhower voiced his disagreements with the Democratic party and declared himself and his family to be Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> A "<a href="/wiki/Draft_Eisenhower" title="Draft Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect">Draft Eisenhower</a>" movement in the Republican Party persuaded him to declare his candidacy in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1952" title="United States presidential election, 1952">1952 presidential election</a> to counter the candidacy of <a href="/wiki/Non-interventionism" title="Non-interventionism">non-interventionist</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft</a>. The effort was a long struggle; Eisenhower had to be convinced that political circumstances had created a genuine duty for him to offer himself as a candidate, and that there was a mandate from the populace for him to be their President. Henry Cabot Lodge, who served as his campaign manager, and others succeeded in convincing him, and in June 1952 he resigned his command at NATO to campaign full-time.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower defeated Taft for the nomination, having won critical delegate votes from Texas. Eisenhower's campaign was noted for the simple but effective slogan, "<a href="/wiki/I_Like_Ike" title="I Like Ike" class="mw-redirect">I Like Ike</a>". It was essential to his success that Eisenhower express opposition to Roosevelt's policy at Yalta and against Truman's policies in Korea and China—matters in which he had once participated.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> In defeating Taft for the nomination, it became necessary for Eisenhower to appease the right wing <a href="/wiki/Old_Right_(United_States)" title="Old Right (United States)">Old Guard</a> of the Republican Party; his selection of <a href="/wiki/Richard_M._Nixon" title="Richard M. Nixon" class="mw-redirect">Richard M. Nixon</a> as the Vice-President on the ticket was designed in part for that purpose. Nixon also provided a strong anti-communist presence as well as some youth to counter Ike's more advanced age.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1952.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ElectoralCollege1952.svg/275px-ElectoralCollege1952.svg.png" width="275" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ElectoralCollege1952.svg/413px-ElectoralCollege1952.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ElectoralCollege1952.svg/550px-ElectoralCollege1952.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="995" data-file-height="572" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1952.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1952 electoral vote results</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the general election, against the advice of his advisors, Eisenhower insisted on campaigning in the South, refusing to surrender the region to the Democratic Party. The campaign strategy, dubbed "K<sub>1</sub>C<sub>2</sub>", was to focus on attacking the Truman and Roosevelt administrations on three issues: Korea, Communism and corruption. In an effort to accommodate the right, he stressed that the liberation of Eastern Europe should be by peaceful means only; he also distanced himself from his former boss President Truman.</p>
<p>Two controversies during the campaign tested him and his staff, but did not affect the campaign. One involved a report that Nixon had improperly received funds from a secret trust. Nixon <a href="/wiki/Checkers_speech" title="Checkers speech">spoke out adroitly</a> to avoid potential damage, but the matter permanently alienated the two candidates. The second issue centered on Eisenhower's relented decision to confront the controversial methods of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a> on his home turf in a Wisconsin appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Just two weeks prior to the election, Eisenhower vowed to go to Korea and end the war there. He promised to maintain a strong commitment against Communism while avoiding the topic of NATO; finally, he stressed a corruption-free, frugal administration at home.</p>
<p>He defeated Democratic candidate <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a> in a landslide, with an electoral margin of 442 to 89, marking the first Republican return to the White House in 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> In the election he also brought with him a Republican majority in the House (by eight votes) and in the Senate (actually a tie, with Nixon providing the majority vote).<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower was the last president born in the 19th century, and at age 62, was the oldest man elected President since <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> in 1856. (President Truman stood at 64 in 1948 as the incumbent president at the time of his election four years earlier.)<sup id="cite_ref-.2770s_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.2770s-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower was the only general to serve as President in the 20th century and the most recent President to have never held elected office prior to the Presidency. (The other Presidents who did not have prior elected office were <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>.)</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281953.E2.80.931961.29">Presidency (1953–1961)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Presidency (1953–1961)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower">Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower</a></div>
<p>Due to a complete estrangement between the two as a result of campaigning, Truman and Eisenhower had minimal discussions about the transition of administrations.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> After selecting his budget director, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Dodge" title="Joseph Dodge">Joseph M. Dodge</a>, Eisenhower asked <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Brownell" title="Herbert Brownell" class="mw-redirect">Herbert Brownell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_D._Clay" title="Lucius D. Clay">Lucius Clay</a> to make recommendations for his cabinet appointments. He accepted their recommendations without exception; they included <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_M._Humphrey" title="George M. Humphrey">George M. Humphrey</a> with whom he developed his closest relationships, and one woman, <a href="/wiki/Oveta_Culp_Hobby" title="Oveta Culp Hobby">Oveta Culp Hobby</a>. Eisenhower's cabinet, consisting of several corporate executives and one labor leader, was dubbed by one journalist, "Eight millionaires and a plumber."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> The cabinet was notable for its lack of personal friends, office seekers, or experienced government administrators. He also upgraded the role of the National Security Council in planning all phases of the Cold War.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:161px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_official.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Eisenhower_official.jpg/159px-Eisenhower_official.jpg" width="159" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Eisenhower_official.jpg/239px-Eisenhower_official.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Eisenhower_official.jpg/318px-Eisenhower_official.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1663" data-file-height="2100" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_official.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The president's official photograph</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prior to his inauguration, he led a meeting of advisors at Pearl Harbor addressing foremost issues; agreed objectives were to balance the budget during his term, to bring the Korean War to an end, to defend vital interests at lower cost through nuclear deterrent, and to end price and wage controls.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower also conducted the first pre-inaugural cabinet meeting in history in late 1952; he used this meeting to articulate his anti-communist Russia policy. His inaugural address as well was exclusively devoted to foreign policy and included this same philosophy as well as a commitment to foreign trade and the U. N.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower made greater use of press conferences than any prior president, holding almost 200 in his two terms. While he saw a positive relationship with the press as invaluable, his primary objective in press conferences was to maintain an indispensable direct contact with the people.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout his presidency, Eisenhower adhered to a political philosophy of dynamic conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> He continued all the major <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> programs still in operation, especially <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a>. He expanded its programs and rolled them into a new cabinet-level agency, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Health,_Education,_and_Welfare" title="United States Department of Health, Education, and Welfare" class="mw-redirect">Department of Health, Education and Welfare</a>, while extending benefits to an additional ten million workers. He implemented integration in the Armed Services in two years, which had not been completed under Truman.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the 1954 congressional elections approached, and it became evident that the Republicans were in danger of losing their thin majority in both houses, Eisenhower was among those blaming the Old Guard for the losses, and took up the charge to stop suspected efforts by the right wing to take control of the GOP. Eisenhower then articulated his position as a moderate, progressive Republican: "I have just one purpose&#160;... and that is to build up a strong progressive Republican Party in this country. If the right wing wants a fight, they are going to get it&#160;... before I end up, either this Republican Party will reflect progressivism or I won't be with them anymore."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Initially Eisenhower planned on serving only one term, but as with other decisions he maintained a position of maximum flexibility in case leading Republicans wanted him to run again. During his recovery from a heart attack late in 1955, he huddled with his closest advisors to evaluate the GOP's potential candidates; the group, in addition to his doctor, concluded a second term was well advised, and he announced in February 1956 he would run again.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower was publicly noncommittal about Nixon's repeating as the Vice President on his ticket; the question was an especially important one in light of his heart condition. He personally favored <a href="/wiki/Robert_B._Anderson" title="Robert B. Anderson">Robert B. Anderson</a>, a Democrat, who rejected his offer; Eisenhower then resolved to leave the matter in the hands of the party.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1956, Eisenhower faced Adlai Stevenson again and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1956" title="United States presidential election, 1956">won by an even larger landslide</a>, with 457 of 531 electoral votes and 57.6% of the popular vote. The level of campaigning was curtailed out of health considerations.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower valued the brief respites and the amenities of an office, which he endowed with an arduous daily schedule. He made full use of his valet, chauffeur, and secretarial support—he rarely drove or dialed a phone number. He was an avid fisherman, golfer, painter, and bridge player, and preferred active rather than passive forms of entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> On August 26, 1959, Eisenhower was aboard the maiden flight of <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a>, which replaced the previous Presidential aircraft, the <i>Columbine</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstate_Highway_System">Interstate Highway System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Interstate Highway System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a></div>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">

</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>One of Eisenhower's enduring achievements was championing and signing the bill that authorized the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a> in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-economist_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> He justified the project through the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aid_Highway_Act_of_1956" title="Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956">Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956</a> as essential to American security during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. It was believed that large cities would be targets in a possible war, hence the highways were designed to facilitate their evacuation and ease military maneuvers.</p>
<p>Eisenhower's goal to create improved highways was influenced by difficulties encountered during his involvement in the U.S. Army's 1919 <a href="/wiki/Transcontinental_Motor_Convoy" title="Transcontinental Motor Convoy">Transcontinental Motor Convoy</a>. He was assigned as an observer for the mission, which involved sending a convoy of U.S. Army vehicles coast to coast.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> His subsequent experience with encountering German <a href="/wiki/Reichsautobahn" title="Reichsautobahn">autobahn</a> <a href="/wiki/Controlled-access_highway" title="Controlled-access highway">limited-access road systems</a> during <a href="/wiki/End_of_World_War_II_in_Europe" title="End of World War II in Europe">the concluding stages of World War II</a> convinced him of the benefits of an Interstate Highway System. Noticing the improved ability to move logistics throughout the country, he thought an Interstate Highway System in the U.S. would not only be beneficial for military operations, but provide a measure of continued economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> The legislation initially stalled in the Congress over the issuance of bonds to finance the project, but the legislative effort was renewed and the law was signed by Eisenhower in June 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Indo_US.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Indo_US.jpg/220px-Indo_US.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Indo_US.jpg/330px-Indo_US.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Indo_US.jpg/440px-Indo_US.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="402" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Indo_US.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower with Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg/330px-U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. President Eisenhower visits <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and its <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="President of the Republic of China">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a> at Taipei</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1953, the Republican Party's Old Guard presented Eisenhower with a dilemma by insisting he disavow the Yalta Agreements as beyond the constitutional authority of the Executive Branch; however, the death of Joseph Stalin in March 1953 made the matter a practical moot point.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> At this time Eisenhower gave his <a href="/wiki/Chance_for_Peace_speech" title="Chance for Peace speech">Chance for Peace speech</a> in which he attempted, unsuccessfully, to forestall the nuclear arms race with the Soviet Union by suggesting multiple opportunities presented by peaceful uses of nuclear materials. Biographer Stephen Ambrose opined that this was the best speech of Eisenhower's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> escalated during his presidency. When the Soviet Union <a href="/wiki/RDS-37" title="RDS-37">successfully tested a hydrogen bomb</a> in late November 1955, Eisenhower, against the advice of Dulles, decided to initiate a disarmament proposal to the Soviets. In an attempt to make their refusal more difficult, he proposed that both sides agree to dedicate fissionable material away from weapons toward peaceful uses, such as power generation. This approach was labeled "<a href="/wiki/Atoms_for_Peace" title="Atoms for Peace">Atoms for Peace</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The U.N. speech was well received but the Soviets never acted upon it, due to an overarching concern for the greater stockpiles of nuclear weapons in the U.S. arsenal. Indeed, Eisenhower embarked upon a greater reliance on the use of nuclear weapons, while reducing conventional forces, and with them the overall defense budget, a policy formulated as a result of <a href="/wiki/Project_Solarium" title="Project Solarium">Project Solarium</a> and expressed in <a href="/wiki/NSC_162/2" title="NSC 162/2">NSC 162/2</a>. This approach became known as the "<a href="/wiki/New_Look_(policy)" title="New Look (policy)">New Look</a>", and was initiated with defense cuts in late 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1955 American nuclear arms policy became one aimed primarily at arms control as opposed to disarmament. The failure of negotiations over arms until 1955 was due mainly to the refusal of the Russians to permit any sort of inspections. In talks located in London that year, they expressed a willingness to discuss inspections; the tables were then turned on Eisenhower, when he responded with an unwillingness on the part of the U.S. to permit inspections. In May of that year the Russians agreed to sign a treaty giving independence to Austria, and paved the way for a Geneva summit with the U.S., U.K. and France.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> At the Geneva Conference Eisenhower presented a proposal called "Open Skies" to facilitate disarmament, which included plans for Russia and the U.S. to provide mutual access to each other's skies for open surveillance of military infrastructure. Russian leader Nikita Khrushchev dismissed the proposal out of hand.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1954, Eisenhower articulated the <a href="/wiki/Domino_theory" title="Domino theory">domino theory</a> in his outlook towards communism in Southeast Asia and also in Central America. He believed that if the communists were allowed to prevail in Vietnam, this would cause a succession of countries to fall to communism, from Laos through Malaysia and Indonesia ultimately to India. Likewise, the fall of Guatemala would end with the fall of neighboring Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> That year the loss of North Vietnam to the communists and the rejection of his proposed <a href="/wiki/European_Defence_Community" title="European Defence Community">European Defence Community</a> (EDC) were serious defeats, but he remained optimistic in his opposition to the spread of communism, saying "Long faces don't win wars".<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> As he had threatened the French in their rejection of EDC, he afterwards moved to restore Germany, as a full NATO partner.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With Eisenhower's leadership and Dulles' direction, CIA activities increased, to resist the spread of communism in poorer countries;<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> the CIA in part deposed the leaders of Iran in <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d'état">Operation Ajax</a>, of Guatemala through <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d'état">Operation Pbsuccess</a>, and possibly the newly independent <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Republic of the Congo (Léopoldville)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1954 Eisenhower wanted to increase surveillance inside the Soviet Union. With Dulles' recommendation, he authorized the deployment of thirty <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a>'s at a cost of $35 million.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> The Eisenhower administration also planned the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a> to overthrow <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, which <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> was left to carry out."<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Call-sign_Air_Force_One">Call-sign Air Force One</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Call-sign Air Force One">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Over <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> in 1953, Eastern Airlines Flight 8610, a commercial flight, had a near miss with Air Force Flight 8610, a Lockheed C-121 Constellation known as <a href="/wiki/Columbine_II" title="Columbine II">Columbine II</a>, while the latter was carrying President Eisenhower, prompting the adoption of the unique call sign <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a> whenever the president was on board any <a href="/wiki/US_Air_Force" title="US Air Force" class="mw-redirect">US Air Force</a> aircraft. Columbine II is the only presidential aircraft to have ever been sold to the public and is the only remaining presidential aircraft left unrestored and not on public display.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_race">Space race</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Space race">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Space_race" title="Space race" class="mw-redirect">Space race</a></div>
<p>On the whole, Eisenhower's support of the nation's fledgling space program was modest until the Soviet launch of <a href="/wiki/Sputnik" title="Sputnik" class="mw-redirect">Sputnik</a> in 1957, gaining the Cold War enemy enormous prestige around the world. He then launched a national campaign that funded not just space exploration but a major strengthening of science and higher education. He rushed construction of more advanced satellites, created <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> as a civilian space agency, signed a landmark science education law, and fostered improved relations with American scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In strategic terms, it was Eisenhower who devised the American basic strategy of nuclear deterrence based upon the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_triad" title="Nuclear triad">triad</a> of B-52 bombers, land-based intercontinental ballistic missiles (ICBMs), and Polaris submarine-launched ballistic missiles (SLBMs).<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_War.2C_China.2C_and_Taiwan">Korean War, China, and Taiwan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Korean War, China, and Taiwan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg/250px-1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg" width="250" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower in Korea with General <a href="/wiki/Chung_Il-kwon" title="Chung Il-kwon">Chung Il-kwon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baik_Seon-yup" title="Baik Seon-yup" class="mw-redirect">Baik Seon-yup</a>, 1952.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In late 1952 Eisenhower went to Korea and discovered a military and political stalemate. Once in office, when the Chinese began a buildup in the Kaesong sanctuary, he threatened to use nuclear force if an armistice was not concluded. His earlier military reputation in Europe was effective with the Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> The National Security Council, the Joint Chiefs of Staff, and the Strategic Air Command (SAC) devised detailed plans for nuclear war against China.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> With the death of Stalin in early March 1953, Russian support for a Chinese hard-line weakened and China decided to compromise on the prisoner issue.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._106.E2.80.937_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._106.E2.80.937-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In July 1953, an armistice took effect with Korea divided along <a href="/wiki/Military_Demarcation_Line" title="Military Demarcation Line">approximately the same boundary as in 1950</a>. The armistice and boundary remain in effect today, with American soldiers stationed there to guarantee it. The armistice, concluded despite opposition from Secretary Dulles, South Korean President <a href="/wiki/Syngman_Rhee" title="Syngman Rhee">Syngman Rhee</a>, and also within Eisenhower's party, has been described by biographer Ambrose as the greatest achievement of the administration. Eisenhower had the insight to realize that unlimited war in the nuclear age was unthinkable, and limited war unwinnable.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._106.E2.80.937_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._106.E2.80.937-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A point of emphasis in Ike's campaign had been his endorsement of a policy of liberation from communism as opposed to a policy of containment. This remained his preference despite the armistice with Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout his terms Eisenhower took a hard-line attitude toward China, as demanded by conservative Republicans, with the goal of driving a wedge between China and the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower continued Truman's policy of recognizing the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">Republic of China</a> (based in <a href="/wiki/Formosa" title="Formosa" class="mw-redirect">Formosa</a>/<a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>) as the legitimate government of China, not the Beijing regime. There were localized flare-ups when the Red Army began shelling the islands of <a href="/wiki/Quemoy" title="Quemoy" class="mw-redirect">Quemoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matsu_Islands" title="Matsu Islands">Matsu</a> in September 1954. Eisenhower received recommendations embracing every variation of response to the aggression of the Chinese communists. He thought it essential to have every possible option available to him as the crisis unfolded.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._231_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._231-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Sino-American Mutual Defense Treaty with Taiwan was signed in December 1954. He requested and secured from Congress their "Formosa Resolution" in January 1955, which gave Eisenhower unprecedented power in advance to use military force at any level of his choosing in defense of Formoso and the Pescadores. The Resolution bolstered the morale of the Chinese nationalists, and signaled to Beijing that the U.S. was committed to holding the line.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._231_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984.2C_p._231-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower openly threatened the Chinese with use of nuclear weapons, authorizing a series of bomb tests labeled <a href="/wiki/Operation_Teapot" title="Operation Teapot">Operation Teapot</a>. Nevertheless, he left the Chinese communists guessing as to the exact nature of his nuclear response. This allowed Eisenhower to accomplish all of his objectives—the end of this communist encroachment, the retention of the Islands by the Chinese nationalists and continued peace.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Defense of Taiwan from an invasion remains a core American policy.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of 1954 Eisenhower's military and foreign policy experts—the NSC, JCS and State Dept.—had unanimously urged him, on no less than five occasions, to launch an atomic attack against China; yet he consistently refused to do so and felt a distinct sense of accomplishment in having sufficiently confronted communism while keeping world peace.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Middle_East_and_Eisenhower_doctrine">The Middle East and Eisenhower doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The Middle East and Eisenhower doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ShahEisenhower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ShahEisenhower.jpg/200px-ShahEisenhower.jpg" width="200" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ShahEisenhower.jpg/300px-ShahEisenhower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/ShahEisenhower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="491" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ShahEisenhower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower with the Shah <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Pahlavi</a> of Iran, 1959.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Even before he was inaugurated Eisenhower accepted a request from the British government to restore the Shah (<a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Pahlavi</a>) to power. He therefore <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d'état">authorized the Central Intelligence Agency</a> to overthrow Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Mosaddegh" title="Mohammad Mosaddegh">Mohammad Mosaddegh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> This resulted in an increased strategic control over Iranian oil by U.S. and British companies.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1956, Eisenhower forced an end to the combined British, French and Israeli invasion of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in response to the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>, receiving praise from Egyptian president <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>. Simultaneously he condemned the brutal Soviet invasion of <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_People%27s_Republic" title="Hungarian People's Republic">Hungary</a> in response to the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956" title="Hungarian Revolution of 1956">Hungarian Revolution of 1956</a>. He publicly disavowed his allies at the United Nations, and used financial and diplomatic pressure to make them withdraw from Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower explicitly defended his strong position against Britain and France in his memoirs, which were published in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg/250px-Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg" width="250" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg/375px-Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg/500px-Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="635" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower and Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> with their host, King <a href="/wiki/Saud_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Saud of Saudi Arabia">Saud of Saudi Arabia</a>, Washington 1957.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the Suez Crisis the United States became the protector of unstable friendly governments in the Middle East via the "<a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Doctrine" title="Eisenhower Doctrine">Eisenhower Doctrine</a>". Designed by Secretary of State Dulles, it held the U.S. would be "prepared to use armed force&#160;... [to counter] aggression from any country controlled by international communism". Further, the United States would provide economic and military aid and, if necessary, use military force to stop the spread of communism in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower applied the doctrine in 1957–58 by dispensing economic aid to shore up the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, and by encouraging <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>'s neighbors to consider military operations against it. More dramatically, in July 1958, he sent 15,000 Marines and soldiers to <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Blue_Bat" title="Operation Blue Bat" class="mw-redirect">Operation Blue Bat</a>, a non-combat peace-keeping mission to stabilize the pro-Western government and to prevent a radical revolution from sweeping over that country.<sup id="cite_ref-Little_1996_27.E2.80.9354_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Little_1996_27.E2.80.9354-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The mission proved a success and the Marines departed three months later. The deployment came in response to the urgent request of Lebanese president <a href="/wiki/Camille_Chamoun" title="Camille Chamoun">Camille Chamoun</a> after sectarian violence had erupted in the country. Washington considered the military intervention successful since it brought about regional stability, weakened Soviet influence, and intimidated the Egyptian and Syrian governments, whose anti-West political position had hardened after the Suez Crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-Little_1996_27.E2.80.9354_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Little_1996_27.E2.80.9354-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Most Arab countries were skeptical about the "Eisenhower doctrine" because they considered "Zionist imperialism" the real danger. However, they did take the opportunity to obtain free money and weapons. Egypt and Syria, supported by the Soviet Union, openly opposed the initiative. However, Egypt received American aid until the <a href="/wiki/Six_Day_War" title="Six Day War" class="mw-redirect">Six Day War</a> in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> deepened, Dulles sought to isolate the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> by building regional alliances of nations against it. Critics sometimes called it "<a href="/wiki/Pactomania" title="Pactomania">pacto-mania</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Early in 1953, the French asked Eisenhower for help in <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a> against the Communists, supplied from China, who were fighting the <a href="/wiki/First_Indochina_War" title="First Indochina War">First Indochina War</a>. Eisenhower sent Lt. General <a href="/wiki/John_W._O%27Daniel" title="John W. O'Daniel">John W. "Iron Mike" O'Daniel</a> to <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> to study and assess the French forces there.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> Chief of Staff <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Ridgway" title="Matthew Ridgway">Matthew Ridgway</a> dissuaded the President from intervening by presenting a comprehensive estimate of the massive military deployment that would be necessary. Eisenhower stated prophetically that "this war would absorb our troops by divisions."<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower did provide France with bombers and non-combat personnel. After a few months with no success by the French, he added other aircraft to drop napalm for clearing purposes. Further requests for assistance from the French were agreed to but only on conditions Eisenhower knew were impossible to meet&#160;– allied participation and congressional approval.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> When the French fortress of <a href="/wiki/Dien_Bien_Phu" title="Dien Bien Phu" class="mw-redirect">Dien Bien Phu</a> fell to the Vietnamese Communists in May 1954, Eisenhower refused to intervene despite urgings from the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs, the Vice President and the head of NCS.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower responded to the French defeat with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/SEATO" title="SEATO" class="mw-redirect">SEATO (Southeast Asia Treaty Organization) Alliance</a> with the U.K., France, New Zealand and Australia in defense of Vietnam against communism. At that time the French and Chinese reconvened Geneva peace talks; Eisenhower agreed the U.S. would participate only as an observer. After France and the Communists agreed to a partition of Vietnam, Eisenhower rejected the agreement, offering military and economic aid to southern Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-Nofi_Albert_p_257_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi_Albert_p_257-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> Ambrose argues that Eisenhower, by not participating in the Geneva agreement, had kept the U.S out of Vietnam; nevertheless, with the formation of SEATO, he had in the end put the U.S. back into the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1954, <a href="/wiki/J._Lawton_Collins" title="J. Lawton Collins">Gen. J. Lawton Collins</a> was made ambassador to "Free Vietnam" (the term <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a> came into use in 1955), effectively elevating the country to sovereign status. Collins' instructions were to support the leader <a href="/wiki/Ngo_Dinh_Diem" title="Ngo Dinh Diem">Ngo Dinh Diem</a> in subverting communism, by helping him to build an army and wage a military campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> In February 1955, Eisenhower dispatched the first American soldiers to Vietnam as military advisors to Diem's army. After Diem announced the formation of the Republic of Vietnam (RVN, commonly known as South Vietnam) in October, Eisenhower immediately recognized the new state and offered military, economic, and technical assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the years that followed, Eisenhower increased the number of U.S. military advisors in South Vietnam to 900 men.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> This was due to <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a>'s support of "uprisings" in the south and concern the nation would fall.<sup id="cite_ref-Nofi_Albert_p_257_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi_Albert_p_257-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 1957 Diem, then <a href="/wiki/Leaders_of_South_Vietnam" title="Leaders of South Vietnam">President of South Vietnam</a>, made a <a href="/wiki/State_visit" title="State visit">state visit</a> to the United States for ten days. President Eisenhower pledged his continued support, and a parade was held in Diem's honor in New York City. Although Diem was publicly praised, in private Secretary of State John Foster Dulles conceded that Diem had been selected because there were no better alternatives.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the election of November 1960, Eisenhower in briefing with <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> pointed out the communist threat in Southeast Asia as requiring prioritization in the next administration. Eisenhower told Kennedy he considered <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> "the cork in the bottle" with regard to the regional threat.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1960_U-2_incident">1960 U-2 incident</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 1960 U-2 incident">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1960_U-2_incident" title="1960 U-2 incident">1960 U-2 incident</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Air_Force_U-2_(2139646280).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg/250px-US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg" width="250" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg/375px-US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg/500px-US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Air_Force_U-2_(2139646280).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">U-2</a> reconnaissance aircraft in flight.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On May 1, 1960, a U.S. one-man <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">U-2</a> spy plane was reportedly shot down at high altitude over <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> airspace. The flight was made to gain photo intelligence before the scheduled opening of an East–West summit conference, which had been scheduled in Paris, 15 days later.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> Captain <a href="/wiki/Francis_Gary_Powers" title="Francis Gary Powers">Francis Gary Powers</a> had bailed out of his aircraft and was captured after parachuting down onto Russian soil. Four days after Powers disappeared, the Eisenhower Administration had NASA issue a very detailed press release noting that an aircraft had "gone missing" north of Turkey. It speculated that the pilot might have fallen unconscious while the autopilot was still engaged, and falsely claimed that "the pilot reported over the emergency frequency that he was experiencing oxygen difficulties."<sup id="cite_ref-Russian_officer_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russian_officer-181"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Soviet Premier <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> announced that a "spy-plane" had been shot down but intentionally made no reference to the pilot. As a result, the Eisenhower Administration, thinking the pilot had died in the crash, authorized the release of a cover story claiming that the plane was a "weather research aircraft" which had unintentionally strayed into Soviet airspace after the pilot had radioed "difficulties with his oxygen equipment" while flying over Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> The Soviets put Captain Powers on trial and displayed parts of the U-2, which had been recovered almost fully intact.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cold_War_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cold_War-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The 1960 Four Power Paris Summit between President Dwight Eisenhower, Nikita Khrushchev, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> collapsed because of the incident. Eisenhower refused to accede to Khrushchev's demands that he apologize. Therefore, Khrushchev would not take part in the summit. Up until this event, Eisenhower felt he had been making progress towards better relations with the Soviet Union. Nuclear arms reduction and Berlin were to have been discussed at the summit. Eisenhower stated it had all been ruined because of that "stupid U-2 business".<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cold_War_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cold_War-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The affair was an embarrassment for United States prestige. Further, the Senate Foreign Relations Committee held a lengthy inquiry into the U-2 incident.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cold_War_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cold_War-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> In Russia, Captain Powers made a forced confession and apology. On August 19, 1960, Powers was convicted of espionage and sentenced to imprisonment. On February 10, 1962, Powers was exchanged for <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Abel" title="Rudolf Abel">Rudolf Abel</a> in Berlin and returned to the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-Russian_officer_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russian_officer-181"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While President Truman had begun the process of <a href="/wiki/Desegregation" title="Desegregation">desegregating</a> the <a href="/wiki/Armed_Forces_of_the_United_States" title="Armed Forces of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Armed Forces</a> in 1948, actual implementation had been slow. Eisenhower made clear his stance in his first <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" title="State of the Union address" class="mw-redirect">State of the Union address</a> in February 1953, saying "I propose to use whatever authority exists in the office of the President to end segregation in the District of Columbia, including the Federal Government, and any segregation in the Armed Forces".<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> When he encountered opposition from the services, he used government control of military spending to force the change through, stating "Wherever Federal Funds are expended&#160;..., I do not see how any American can justify&#160;... a discrimination in the expenditure of those funds".<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Robert_B._Anderson" title="Robert B. Anderson">Robert B. Anderson</a>, Eisenhower's first <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Secretary of the Navy" class="mw-redirect">Secretary of the Navy</a>, argued that the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" title="U.S. Navy" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Navy</a> must recognize the "customs and usages prevailing in certain geographic areas of our country which the Navy had no part in creating," Eisenhower overruled him: "We have not taken and we shall not take a single backward step. There must be no second class citizens in this country."<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The administration declared <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination" class="mw-redirect">racial discrimination</a> a <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a> issue, as Communists around the world used the racial discrimination and history of violence in the U.S. as a point of propaganda attack.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eisenhower told <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia" class="mw-redirect">District of Columbia</a> officials to make Washington a model for the rest of the country in integrating black and white public school children.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> He proposed to Congress the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1957" title="Civil Rights Act of 1957">Civil Rights Act of 1957</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1960" title="Civil Rights Act of 1960">of 1960</a> and signed those acts into law. The 1957 act for the first time established a permanent civil rights office inside the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Commission" title="Civil Rights Commission" class="mw-redirect">Civil Rights Commission</a> to hear testimony about abuses of voting rights. Although both acts were much weaker than subsequent civil rights legislation, they constituted the first significant civil rights acts <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">since 1875</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1957, the state of <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> refused to honor a federal court order to integrate their public school system stemming from the <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown</a></i> decision. Eisenhower demanded that Arkansas governor <a href="/wiki/Orval_Faubus" title="Orval Faubus">Orval Faubus</a> obey the court order. When Faubus balked, the president placed the <a href="/wiki/Arkansas_Army_National_Guard" title="Arkansas Army National Guard">Arkansas National Guard</a> under federal control and sent in the <a href="/wiki/101st_Airborne_Division" title="101st Airborne Division">101st Airborne Division</a>. They escorted and protected <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Nine" title="Little Rock Nine">nine black students</a>' entry to <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Central_High_School" title="Little Rock Central High School">Little Rock Central High School</a>, an all-white public school, for the first time since the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr." class="mw-redirect">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> wrote to Eisenhower to thank him for his actions, writing "The overwhelming majority of southerners, Negro and white, stand firmly behind your resolute action to restore law and order in Little Rock".<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_Congress">Relations with Congress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Relations with Congress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Eisenhower had a Republican Congress for only his first two years in office; in the Senate, the Republican majority was by a one-vote margin. Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft</a> assisted the President greatly in working with the Old Guard, and was sorely missed when his death (in July 1953) left Eisenhower with his successor <a href="/wiki/William_Knowland" title="William Knowland" class="mw-redirect">William Knowland</a>, whom Eisenhower disliked.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>This prevented Eisenhower from openly condemning Joseph McCarthy's highly criticized methods against communism. To facilitate relations with Congress, Eisenhower decided to ignore McCarthy's controversies and thereby deprive them of more energy from involvement of the White House. This position drew criticism from a number of corners.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> In late 1953 McCarthy declared on national television that the employment of communists within the government was a menace and would be a pivotal issue in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_elections,_1954" title="United States Senate elections, 1954">1954 Senate elections</a>. Eisenhower was urged to respond directly and specify the various measures he had taken to purge the government of communists.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, he refused.</p>
<p>Among Ike's objectives in not directly confronting McCarthy was to prevent McCarthy from dragging the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a> (AEC) into McCarthy's witch hunt for communists, which would interfere with, and perhaps delay, the AEC's important work on <a href="/wiki/H-bomb" title="H-bomb" class="mw-redirect">H-bombs</a>. The administration had discovered through its own investigations that one of the leading scientists on the AEC, <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">J. Robert Oppenheimer</a>, had urged that the H-bomb work be delayed. Eisenhower removed him from the agency and revoked his security clearance, though he knew this would create fertile ground for McCarthy.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1955, McCarthy threatened to issue subpoenas to White House personnel. Eisenhower was furious, and issued an order as follows: "It is essential to efficient and effective administration that employees of the Executive Branch be in a position to be completely candid in advising with each other on official matters&#160;... it is not in the public interest that any of their conversations or communications, or any documents or reproductions, concerning such advice be disclosed." This was an unprecedented step by Eisenhower to protect communication beyond the confines of a cabinet meeting, and soon became a tradition known as <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">Executive privilege</a>. Ike's denial of McCarthy's access to his staff reduced McCarthy's hearings to rants about trivial matters, and contributed to his ultimate downfall.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1954, the Old Guard put forward a constitutional amendment, called the <a href="/wiki/Bricker_Amendment" title="Bricker Amendment">Bricker Amendment</a>, which would curtail international agreements by the Chief Executive, such as the <a href="/wiki/Yalta_Agreement" title="Yalta Agreement" class="mw-redirect">Yalta Agreements</a>. Eisenhower opposed the measure.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> The Old Guard agreed with Eisenhower on the development and ownership of nuclear reactors by private enterprises, which the Democrats opposed. The President succeeded in getting legislation creating a system of licensure for nuclear plants by the AEC.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats gained a majority in both houses in the 1954 election.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower had to work with the Democratic Majority Leader <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> (later U.S. president) in the Senate and Speaker <a href="/wiki/Sam_Rayburn" title="Sam Rayburn">Sam Rayburn</a> in the House, both from Texas. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_W._Martin_Jr." title="Joseph W.
