Martin Jr." class="mw-redirect">Joe Martin</a>, the Republican Speaker from 1947 to 1949 and again from 1953 to 1955, wrote that Eisenhower "never surrounded himself with assistants who could solve political problems with professional skill. There were exceptions, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_W._Hall" title="Leonard W. Hall">Leonard W. Hall</a>, for example, who as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> tried to open the administration's eyes to the political facts of life, with occasional success. However, these exceptions were not enough to right the balance."<sup id="cite_ref-martin_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Speaker Martin concluded that Eisenhower worked too much through subordinates in dealing with Congress, with results, "often the reverse of what he has desired" because Members of Congress, "resent having some young fellow who was picked up by the White House without ever having been elected to office himself coming around and telling them 'The Chief wants this'. The administration never made use of many Republicans of consequence whose services in one form or another would have been available for the asking."<sup id="cite_ref-martin_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supreme_Court">Supreme Court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Supreme Court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_Supreme_Court_candidates" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower Supreme Court candidates">Dwight D. Eisenhower Supreme Court candidates</a></div>
<p>Eisenhower appointed the following <a href="/wiki/List_of_Justices_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="List of Justices of the Supreme Court of the United States">Justices</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>, 1953 (Chief Justice)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_Harlan_II" title="John Marshall Harlan II">John Marshall Harlan II</a>, 1954</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_J._Brennan" title="William J. Brennan" class="mw-redirect">William J. Brennan</a>, 1956</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Whittaker" title="Charles Evans Whittaker">Charles Evans Whittaker</a>, 1957</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Potter_Stewart" title="Potter Stewart">Potter Stewart</a>, 1958</li>
</ul>
<p>Whittaker was unsuited for the role and soon retired. Stewart and Harlan were conservative Republicans, while Brennan was a Democrat who became a leading voice for liberalism.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> In selecting a Chief Justice Eisenhower looked for an experienced jurist who could appeal to liberals in the party as well as law-and-order conservatives, noting privately that Warren "represents the kind of political, economic, and social thinking that I believe we need on the Supreme Court&#160;... He has a national name for integrity, uprightness, and courage that, again, I believe we need on the Court".<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> In the next few years Warren led the Court in a series of liberal decisions that revolutionized the role of the Court.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_courts">Other courts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other courts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_judicial_appointments" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower judicial appointments" class="mw-redirect">Dwight D. Eisenhower judicial appointments</a></div>
<p>In addition to his five Supreme Court appointments, Eisenhower appointed 45 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Courts_of_Appeals" title="United States Courts of Appeals" class="mw-redirect">United States Courts of Appeals</a>, and 129 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" title="United States district courts" class="mw-redirect">United States district courts</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: States admitted to the Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>&#160;– January 3, 1959 <small>49th state</small></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>&#160;– August 21, 1959 <small>50th state</small></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_issues">Health issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Health issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Eisenhower began smoking cigarettes at West Point, often <a href="/wiki/Chain_smoker" title="Chain smoker" class="mw-redirect">two or three packs a day</a>. Eisenhower stated that he "gave [himself] an order" to stop <a href="/wiki/Cold_turkey" title="Cold turkey">cold turkey</a> in March 1949 while at Columbia.<sup id="cite_ref-smith20123132_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith20123132-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> He was probably the first president to release information about his health and medical records while in office,<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 24, 1955, while vacationing in <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, he had a serious heart attack that required six weeks' hospitalization, during which time Nixon, Dulles, and Sherman Adams assumed administrative duties and provided communication with the President.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> He was treated by Dr. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dudley_White" title="Paul Dudley White">Paul Dudley White</a>, a cardiologist with a national reputation, who regularly informed the press of the President's progress. Instead of eliminating him as a candidate for a second term as President, his physician recommended a second term as essential to his recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As a consequence of his heart attack, Eisenhower developed a left ventricular <a href="/wiki/Aneurysm" title="Aneurysm">aneurysm</a>, which was in turn the cause of a mild stroke on November 25, 1957. This incident occurred during a cabinet meeting when Eisenhower suddenly found himself unable to speak or move his right hand. The stroke had caused an <a href="/wiki/Aphasia" title="Aphasia">aphasia</a>. The president also suffered from <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn's disease">Crohn's disease</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> chronic inflammatory condition of the intestine,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> which necessitated surgery for a bowel obstruction on June 9, 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> To treat the intestinal block, surgeons bypassed about ten inches of his <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> His scheduled meeting with <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_India" title="Prime Minister of India">Indian Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a> was postponed so he could recover from surgery at his farm in <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Gettysburg, Pennsylvania">Gettysburg, Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> He was still recovering from this operation during the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>. Eisenhower's health issues forced him to give up smoking and make some changes to his dietary habits, but he still indulged in alcohol. During a visit to England he complained of dizziness and had to have his blood pressure checked on August 29, 1959; however, before dinner at Chequers on the next day his doctor General Howard Snyder recalled Eisenhower "drank several gin-and-tonics, and one or two gins on the rocks&#160;... three or four wines with the dinner".<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The last three years of Eisenhower's second term in office were ones of relatively good health. Eventually after leaving the White House, he suffered several additional and ultimately crippling heart attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-drzebra_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drzebra-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> A severe heart attack in August 1965 largely ended his participation in public affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> In August 1966 he began to show symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Cholecystitis" title="Cholecystitis">cholecystitis</a>, for which he underwent surgery on December 12, 1966, when his gallbladder was removed, containing 16 gallstones.<sup id="cite_ref-drzebra_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drzebra-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> After Eisenhower's death in 1969 (see below), an autopsy unexpectedly revealed an adrenal <a href="/wiki/Pheochromocytoma" title="Pheochromocytoma">pheochromocytoma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> a benign adrenaline-secreting tumor that may have made the President more vulnerable to heart disease. Eisenhower suffered seven heart attacks in total from 1955 until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-drzebra_214-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drzebra-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_presidency_1960.E2.80.931961">End of presidency 1960–1961</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: End of presidency 1960–1961">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg/225px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg" width="225" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg/338px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg/450px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2345" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The official <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> portrait of Dwight D. Eisenhower.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">22nd Amendment to the U.S. Constitution</a> was ratified in 1951, and it set term limits to the presidency of two terms. It stipulated that <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, the incumbent at the time, would not be affected by the amendment. After he had been elected to his second presidential term in 1956, Eisenhower became the first U.S. president constitutionally prevented from running for re-election to the office, having served the maximum two terms.</p>
<p>Eisenhower was also the first outgoing President to come under the protection of the <a href="/wiki/Former_Presidents_Act" title="Former Presidents Act">Former Presidents Act</a>; two living former Presidents, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, left office before the Act was passed. Under the act, Eisenhower was entitled to receive a lifetime pension, state-provided staff and a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> detail.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1960 election to choose his successor, Eisenhower endorsed his own Vice President, Republican <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> against Democrat <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>. He told friends, "I will do almost anything to avoid turning my chair and country over to Kennedy."<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> He actively campaigned for Nixon in the final days, although he may have done Nixon some harm. When asked by reporters at the end of a televised press conference to list one of Nixon's policy ideas he had adopted, Eisenhower joked, "If you give me a week, I might think of one. I don't remember." Kennedy's campaign used the quote in one of its campaign commercials. Nixon narrowly lost to Kennedy. Eisenhower, who was the oldest president in history at that time (then 70), was succeeded by the youngest elected president, as Kennedy was 43.<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_108-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" style="width:220px" poster="/static/1.27.0-wmf.3/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" controls="" preload="none" class="kskin" data-durationhint="930.26696145125" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (112 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="111848"></source>Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.<br />
You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg">download the clip</a> or <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">download a player</a> to play the clip in your browser.</audio></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower's farewell address, January 17, 1961. Length 15:30.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On January 17, 1961, Eisenhower gave his final televised Address to the Nation from the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DDEFarewell_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DDEFarewell-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower%27s_farewell_address" title="Eisenhower's farewell address">farewell speech</a>, Eisenhower raised the issue of the Cold War and role of the U.S. armed forces. He described the Cold War: "We face a hostile ideology global in scope, atheistic in character, ruthless in purpose and insidious in method&#160;..." and warned about what he saw as unjustified government spending proposals and continued with a warning that "we must guard against the acquisition of unwarranted influence, whether sought or unsought, by the military–industrial complex."<sup id="cite_ref-DDEFarewell_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DDEFarewell-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He elaborated, "we recognize the imperative need for this development&#160;... the potential for the disastrous rise of misplaced power exists and will persist&#160;... Only an alert and knowledgeable citizenry can compel the proper meshing of the huge industrial and military machinery of defense with our peaceful methods and goals, so that security and liberty may prosper together."<sup id="cite_ref-DDEFarewell_218-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DDEFarewell-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Because of legal issues related to holding a military rank while in a civilian office, Eisenhower had resigned his permanent commission as <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a> before entering the office of President of the United States. Upon completion of his Presidential term, his commission was reactivated by Congress and Eisenhower again was commissioned a five-star general in the United States Army.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement.2C_death_and_funeral">Retirement, death and funeral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Retirement, death and funeral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg/250px-LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg" width="250" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg/375px-LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg/500px-LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1364" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower meets with <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">President Johnson</a> in October 1965.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_March_1969.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg/250px-Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg" width="250" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg/375px-Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg/500px-Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1926" data-file-height="1803" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_March_1969.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower's funeral service.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg/220px-DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg/330px-DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg/440px-DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3072" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graves of Dwight D. Eisenhower, Doud Dwight "Icky" Eisenhower and Mamie Eisenhower in Abilene, Kansas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eisenhower retired to the place where he and Mamie had spent much of their post-war time, a working farm adjacent to the battlefield at <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Gettysburg, Pennsylvania">Gettysburg, Pennsylvania</a>, only thirty miles from his ancestral home of York. In 1967 the Eisenhowers donated the farm to the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a>. In retirement, the former president did not completely retreat from political life; he spoke at the <a href="/wiki/1964_Republican_National_Convention" title="1964 Republican National Convention">1964 Republican National Convention</a> and appeared with <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> in a Republican campaign commercial from Gettysburg.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> However, his endorsement came somewhat reluctantly because Goldwater had attacked the former president as "a dime-store New Dealer".</p>
<p>On the morning of March 28, 1969, at the age of 78, Eisenhower died in Washington, D.C. of <a href="/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" title="Congestive heart failure" class="mw-redirect">congestive heart failure</a> at <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed_Army_Hospital" title="Walter Reed Army Hospital" class="mw-redirect">Walter Reed Army Hospital</a>. The following day his body was moved to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">Washington National Cathedral</a>'s Bethlehem Chapel, where he lay in repose for 28 hours. On March 30, his body was brought by <a href="/wiki/Caisson_(military)" title="Caisson (military)" class="mw-redirect">caisson</a> to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>, where he lay in state in the <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Rotunda" title="Capitol Rotunda" class="mw-redirect">Capitol Rotunda</a>. On March 31, Eisenhower's body was returned to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">National Cathedral</a>, where he was given an <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America" title="Episcopal Church in the United States of America" class="mw-redirect">Episcopal Church</a> funeral service.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalPost_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalPost-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>That evening, Eisenhower's body was placed onto a train en route to <a href="/wiki/Abilene,_Kansas" title="Abilene, Kansas">Abilene, Kansas</a>. His body arrived on April 2, and was interred later that day in a small chapel on the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Presidential_Library" title="Eisenhower Presidential Library" class="mw-redirect">Eisenhower Presidential Library</a>. The president's body was buried as a General of the Army. The family used an $80 standard soldier's casket, and dressed Eisenhower's body in his famous <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_jacket" title="Eisenhower jacket">short green jacket</a>. His only medals worn were: the Army Distinguished Service Medal with three oak leaf clusters, the Navy Distinguished Service Medal, and the Legion of Merit. Eisenhower is buried alongside his son Doud, who died at age 3 in 1921. His wife Mamie was buried next to him after her death in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalPost_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalPost-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Richard Nixon, then President, spoke of Eisenhower,</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Some men are considered great because they lead great armies or they lead powerful nations. For eight years now, Dwight Eisenhower has neither commanded an army nor led a nation; and yet he remained through his final days the world's most admired and respected man, truly the first citizen of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_memory">Legacy and memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Legacy and memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the immediate years after Eisenhower left office, his reputation declined. He was widely seen by critics as an inactive, uninspiring president compared to his vigorous young successor. Despite his unprecedented use of Army troops to enforce a federal desegregation order at <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Central_High_School" title="Little Rock Central High School">Central High School</a> in Little Rock, Eisenhower was criticized for his reluctance to support the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights" class="mw-redirect">civil rights</a> movement to the degree that activists wanted. Eisenhower also attracted criticism for his handling of the <a href="/wiki/1960_U-2_incident" title="1960 U-2 incident">1960 U-2 incident</a> and the associated international embarrassment,<sup id="cite_ref-.2770s_27_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.2770s_27-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-us_news_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_news-225"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> for the Soviet Union's perceived leadership in the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_arms_race" title="Nuclear arms race">nuclear arms race</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>, and for his failure to publicly oppose <a href="/wiki/McCarthyism" title="McCarthyism">McCarthyism</a>.</p>
<p>In particular, Eisenhower was criticized for failing to defend <a href="/wiki/George_Marshall" title="George Marshall">George Marshall</a> from attacks by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a>, though he privately deplored McCarthy's tactics and claims.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup> Such omissions were held against him during the <a href="/wiki/American_liberalism" title="American liberalism" class="mw-redirect">liberal</a> climate of the 1960s and 1970s. Since that time, however, Eisenhower's reputation has risen. <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_U.S._Presidents" title="Historical rankings of U.S. Presidents" class="mw-redirect">Recent surveys</a> of historians since the 1980s often rank Eisenhower in the top 10 of all U.S. presidents.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg/250px-72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg" width="250" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg/375px-72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg/500px-72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg 2x" data-file-width="504" data-file-height="401" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
On June 1, 1954, this signing ceremony changed <a href="/wiki/Armistice_Day" title="Armistice Day">Armistice Day</a> to <a href="/wiki/Veterans_Day_(United_States)" title="Veterans Day (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Veterans Day</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/John_Lewis_Gaddis" title="John Lewis Gaddis">John Lewis Gaddis</a> has summarized the turnaround in evaluations by historians:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Historians long ago abandoned the view that Eisenhower's was a failed presidency. He did, after all, end the Korean War without getting into any others. He stabilized, and did not escalate, the Soviet-American rivalry. He strengthened European alliances while withdrawing support from European colonialism. He rescued the Republican Party from isolationism and McCarthyism. He maintained prosperity, balanced the budget, promoted technological innovation, facilitated (if reluctantly) the civil rights movement and warned, in the most memorable farewell address since Washington's, of a "military–industrial complex" that could endanger the nation's liberties. Not until Reagan would another president leave office with so strong a sense of having accomplished what he set out to do.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Although conservatism in politics was strong during the 1950s and Eisenhower generally espoused conservative sentiments, his administration concerned itself mostly with foreign affairs (an area in which the career-military president had more knowledge) and pursued a hands-off domestic policy. Eisenhower looked to moderation and cooperation as a means of governance.<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-228"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although he sought to slow or contain the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> and other federal programs, he did not attempt to repeal them outright, and in doing so was popular among the liberal wing of the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-228"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> Conservative critics of his administration found that he did not do enough to advance the goals of the right; according to <a href="/wiki/Hans_Morgenthau" title="Hans Morgenthau">Hans Morgenthau</a>, "Eisenhower's victories were but accidents without consequence in the history of the Republican party."<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy,_President_Eisenhower,_military_aides._Camp_David,_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg/250px-Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg" width="250" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg/375px-Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg/500px-Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2378" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy,_President_Eisenhower,_military_aides._Camp_David,_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower meets with President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, April 22, 1961.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the 19th century, many if not all presidents were assisted by a central figure or "gatekeeper", sometimes described as the President's Private Secretary, sometimes with no official title at all.<sup id="cite_ref-medved_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medved-230"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> Eisenhower formalized this role, introducing the office of <a href="/wiki/White_House_Chief_of_Staff" title="White House Chief of Staff">White House Chief of Staff</a>&#160;– an idea he borrowed from the United States Army. Every president after <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" title="Lyndon Johnson" class="mw-redirect">Lyndon Johnson</a> has also appointed staff to this position. Initially, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> tried to operate without a chief of staff, but each eventually appointed one.</p>
<p>As President, Eisenhower also initiated the "<a href="/wiki/Up_or_out" title="Up or out">up or out</a>" policy in the U.S. military, whereby officers who are passed over for promotion <i>twice</i> are usually honorably discharged to make way for younger, more able officers. Eisenhower never forgot his own frustration at being stuck at the rank of major for 16 years between the two world wars.</p>
<p>Eisenhower founded <a href="/wiki/People_to_People_International" title="People to People International">People to People International</a> in 1956, based on his belief that citizen interaction would promote cultural interaction and world peace. The program includes a <a href="/wiki/People_to_People_Student_Ambassadors" title="People to People Student Ambassadors" class="mw-redirect">student ambassador component</a>, which sends American youth on educational trips to other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg/200px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg" width="200" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg/300px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg/400px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="610" /></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Frank_Gasparro" title="Frank Gasparro">Frank Gasparro</a>'s obverse design (left) and reverse design (right) of the Presidential Medal of Appreciation award during Eisenhower's official visit to the State of Hawaii from June 20–25, 1960.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his second term as President, Eisenhower distinctively preserved his presidential gratitude by awarding individuals a special memento. This memento was a series of specially designed U.S. Mint presidential appreciation medals. Eisenhower presented the medal as an expression of his appreciation and the medal is a keepsake reminder for the recipient.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-232"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The development of the appreciation medals was initiated by the White House and executed by the Bureau of the Mint through the U.S. Mint in Philadelphia. The medals were struck from September 1958 though October 1960. A total of twenty designs are cataloged with a total mintage of 9,858. Each of the designs incorporates the text "with appreciation" or "with personal and official gratitude" accompanied with Eisenhower's initials "D.D.E." or facsimile signature. The design also incorporates location, date, and/or significant event. Prior to the end of his second term as President, 1,451 medals were turned-in to the Bureau of the Mint and destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_232-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-232"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> The Eisenhower appreciation medals are part of the Presidential Medal of Appreciation Award Medal Series.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_232-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-232"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributes_and_memorials">Tributes and memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Tributes and memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Eisenhower is remembered for his role in World War II, the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a> and ending the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG/250px-Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG/375px-Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG/500px-Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eisenhower Interstate System sign south of <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, Texas.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_bronze.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Eisenhower_bronze.jpg/150px-Eisenhower_bronze.jpg" width="150" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Eisenhower_bronze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Eisenhower_bronze.jpg 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_bronze.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bronze statue of Eisenhower at <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol_rotunda" title="United States Capitol rotunda">Capitol rotunda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Interstate Highway System is officially known as the 'Dwight D. Eisenhower National System of Interstate and Defense Highways' in his honor. It was inspired in part by Eisenhower's own Army experiences in World War Two, where he recognized the advantages of the <a href="/wiki/Autobahn" title="Autobahn">autobahn</a> systems in Germany, Austria, and Switzerland. Commemorative signs reading "Eisenhower Interstate System" and bearing Eisenhower's permanent <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army" title="General of the Army" class="mw-redirect">5-star rank</a> insignia were introduced in 1993 and are currently displayed throughout the Interstate System. Several highways are also named for him, including the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_290_(Illinois)" title="Interstate 290 (Illinois)">Eisenhower Expressway</a> (Interstate 290) near <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Tunnel" title="Eisenhower Tunnel">Eisenhower Tunnel</a> on <a href="/wiki/Interstate_70" title="Interstate 70">Interstate 70</a> west of <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>.</p>
<p>The British A4 class steam locomotive No. 4496 (renumbered 60008) <i>Golden Shuttle</i> was renamed <i><a href="/wiki/LNER_Class_A4_4496_Dwight_D_Eisenhower" title="LNER Class A4 4496 Dwight D Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a></i> in 1946. It is preserved at the <a href="/wiki/National_Railroad_Museum" title="National Railroad Museum">National Railroad Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Green_Bay,_Wisconsin" title="Green Bay, Wisconsin">Green Bay</a>, Wisconsin. <a href="/wiki/USS_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_(CVN-69)" title="USS Dwight D. Eisenhower (CVN-69)" class="mw-redirect">USS <i>Dwight D. Eisenhower</i></a>, the second <a href="/wiki/Nimitz_class_carrier" title="Nimitz class carrier" class="mw-redirect"><i>Nimitz</i>-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Supercarrier" title="Supercarrier">supercarrier</a>, was named in his honor.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_College" title="Eisenhower College">Eisenhower College</a> was a small, liberal arts college chartered in <a href="/wiki/Seneca_Falls_(village),_New_York" title="Seneca Falls (village), New York" class="mw-redirect">Seneca Falls</a>, New York in 1965, with classes beginning in 1968. Financial problems forced the school to fall under the management of the <a href="/wiki/Rochester_Institute_of_Technology" title="Rochester Institute of Technology">Rochester Institute of Technology</a> in 1979. Its last class graduated in 1983.</p>
<p>Eisenhower Hall, the cadet activities building at West Point, was completed in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1983, the <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Monument" title="Eisenhower Monument">Eisenhower Monument</a> was unveiled at West Point.</p>
<p>Eisenhower was honored on a <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_dollar" title="Eisenhower dollar">US one dollar coin</a>, minted from 1971 to 1978. His centenary was honored on a <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_commemorative_dollar" title="Eisenhower commemorative dollar">commemorative dollar coin</a> issued in 1990.</p>
<p>In 1969 four major record companies, ABC Records, MGM Records, Buddha Records and Caedmon released tribute albums in Eisenhower's honour.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Medical_Center" title="Eisenhower Medical Center">Eisenhower Medical Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rancho_Mirage,_California" title="Rancho Mirage, California">Rancho Mirage</a>, California was named after the President in 1971.</p>
<p>The NATO Defense College in Rome has a statue of General Eisenhower as the founder of the College.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_Army_Medical_Center" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower Army Medical Center">Dwight D. Eisenhower Army Medical Center</a>, located at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Gordon" title="Fort Gordon">Fort Gordon</a> near <a href="/wiki/Augusta,_Georgia" title="Augusta, Georgia">Augusta</a>, Georgia, was named in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The transcontinental <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_80" title="Interstate Highway 80" class="mw-redirect">Interstate Highway 80</a> was named after Eisenhower. The <a href="/wiki/Highway" title="Highway">highway</a> stretches from the <a href="/wiki/Bay_Bridge_(California)" title="Bay Bridge (California)" class="mw-redirect">Bay Bridge</a> connecting <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay_Area" title="San Francisco Bay Area">Oakland</a>, all the way to the <a href="/wiki/Suburbs" title="Suburbs" class="mw-redirect">suburbs</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1983, <a href="/wiki/The_Eisenhower_Institute" title="The Eisenhower Institute" class="mw-redirect">The Eisenhower Institute</a> was founded in Washington, D.C., as a policy institute to advance Eisenhower's intellectual and leadership legacies.</p>
<p>In 1989, U.S. Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Charles_H._Price_II" title="Charles H. Price II">Charles Price</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_UK" title="Prime Minister of the UK" class="mw-redirect">British Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> dedicated a bronze statue of Eisenhower in <a href="/wiki/Grosvenor_Square" title="Grosvenor Square">Grosvenor Square</a>, London. The statue is located in front of the current <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_States_in_London" title="Embassy of the United States in London" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Embassy, London</a> and across from the former command center for the Allied Expeditionary Force during World War II, offices Eisenhower occupied during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1999, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_Memorial" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower Memorial">Dwight D. Eisenhower Memorial</a> Commission, to create an enduring <a href="/wiki/National_memorial" title="National memorial" class="mw-redirect">national memorial</a> in Washington, D.C.. In 2009, the commission chose the architect <a href="/wiki/Frank_Gehry" title="Frank Gehry">Frank Gehry</a> to design the memorial.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WPmemorial_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPmemorial-239"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup> The memorial will stand near the <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">National Mall</a> on Maryland Avenue, SW across the street from the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On May 7, 2002, the <a href="/wiki/Old_Executive_Office_Building" title="Old Executive Office Building" class="mw-redirect">Old Executive Office Building</a> was officially renamed the Eisenhower Executive Office Building. This building is part of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House Complex</a>, and is west of the <a href="/wiki/West_Wing" title="West Wing">West Wing</a>. It currently houses a number of executive offices, including ones for the Vice President and his or her spouse.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A county park in <a href="/wiki/East_Meadow,_New_York" title="East Meadow, New York">East Meadow</a>, New York (<a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>) is named in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_State_Park_(Texas)" title="Eisenhower State Park (Texas)">Eisenhower State Park</a> on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Texoma" title="Lake Texoma">Lake Texoma</a> near his birthplace of Denison is named in his honor.</p>
<p>His birthplace is currently operated by the State of Texas as the <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Birthplace_State_Historic_Site" title="Eisenhower Birthplace State Historic Site">Eisenhower Birthplace State Historic Site</a>. Since 1980, the National Park Service has allowed visitors to the Eisenhower Farm adjacent to the <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg_Battlefield" title="Gettysburg Battlefield">Gettysburg Battlefield</a>.</p>
<p>Many public <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_High_School_(disambiguation)" title="Eisenhower High School (disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect">high schools</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Middle_School_(disambiguation)" title="Eisenhower Middle School (disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect">middle schools</a> in the U.S. are named after Eisenhower.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mount_Eisenhower" title="Mount Eisenhower">Mount Eisenhower</a> was named in the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Range" title="Presidential Range">Presidential Range</a> of the <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_(New_Hampshire)" title="White Mountains (New Hampshire)">White Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wichita_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_National_Airport" title="Wichita Dwight D. Eisenhower National Airport">Wichita Dwight D. Eisenhower National Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita, Kansas</a>. The FAA changed the name in 2014 so it would be included in new 2015 maps, but the official dedication will occur in 2015.</p>
<p>The Eisenhower Golf Club at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_Academy" title="United States Air Force Academy">United States Air Force Academy</a>, a 36-hole facility featuring the Blue and Silver courses, which is ranked No. 1 among <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">DoD</a> courses, is named in his honor.</p>
<p>Eisenhower Park on Washington Square in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>, dedicated by President Eisenhower in 1960.</p>
<p>The 18th hole at <a href="/wiki/Cherry_Hills_Country_Club" title="Cherry Hills Country Club">Cherry Hills Country Club</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, is named in his honor. Eisenhower was a longtime member of the club, which operated one of his favorite courses.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Loblolly_pine" title="Loblolly pine" class="mw-redirect">loblolly pine</a>, known as the "Eisenhower Pine", was located on Augusta's 17th hole, approximately 210 yards (192 m) from <a href="/wiki/The_Masters" title="The Masters" class="mw-redirect">the Masters</a> tee. President Dwight D. Eisenhower, an <a href="/wiki/Augusta_National" title="Augusta National" class="mw-redirect">Augusta National</a> member, hit the tree so many times that, at a 1956 club meeting, he proposed that it be cut down. Not wanting to offend the president, the club's chairman, <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Roberts" title="Clifford Roberts">Clifford Roberts</a>, immediately adjourned the meeting rather than reject the request. The tree was removed in February 2014 after an ice storm caused it significant damage.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During a visit to Augusta National, then General Eisenhower returned from a walk through the woods on the eastern part of the grounds, and informed <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Roberts" title="Clifford Roberts">Clifford Roberts</a> that he had found a perfect place to build a dam if the club would like a fish pond. Ike's Pond was built and named, and the dam is located just where Eisenhower said it should be.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_decorations">Awards and decorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Awards and decorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Orden-Pobeda-Marshal_Vasilevsky.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Orden-Pobeda-Marshal_Vasilevsky.jpg/220px-Orden-Pobeda-Marshal_Vasilevsky.jpg" width="220" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Orden-Pobeda-Marshal_Vasilevsky.jpg/330px-Orden-Pobeda-Marshal_Vasilevsky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Orden-Pobeda-Marshal_Vasilevsky.jpg/440px-Orden-Pobeda-Marshal_Vasilevsky.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="476" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Dwight_Eisenhower.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Coat_of_Arms_of_Dwight_Eisenhower.svg/220px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Dwight_Eisenhower.svg.png" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Coat_of_Arms_of_Dwight_Eisenhower.svg/330px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Dwight_Eisenhower.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Coat_of_Arms_of_Dwight_Eisenhower.svg/440px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Dwight_Eisenhower.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="498" data-file-height="677" /></a>

The <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a> granted to Eisenhower upon his incorporation as a knight of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Elephant" title="Order of the Elephant">Order of the Elephant</a> in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup> The anvil represents the fact that his name is derived from the German for "iron hewer"</div>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_honors">Other honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Other honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>An apartment at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Culzean_Castle" title="Culzean Castle">Culzean Castle</a> in Scotland was given to General of the Army Dwight D. Eisenhower in recognition of his role as Supreme Commander of the Allied Forces in Europe during the Second World War. The General first visited Culzean Castle in 1946 and stayed there four times, including once while President of the United States. An Eisenhower exhibition occupies one of the rooms, with mementos of his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In January 1946, <a href="/wiki/The_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="The Metropolitan Museum of Art" class="mw-redirect">The Metropolitan Museum of Art</a> named Eisenhower an Honorary Fellow for Life in recognition of his efforts to recover art looted by the Nazis during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In 1966, Eisenhower was the second person awarded <a href="/wiki/Civitan_International" title="Civitan International">Civitan International</a>'s World Citizenship Award.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In December 1999, Eisenhower was listed on <a href="/wiki/Gallup%27s_List_of_Widely_Admired_People" title="Gallup's List of Widely Admired People" class="mw-redirect">Gallup's List of Most Widely Admired People of the 20th century</a>.</li>
<li>In 2009, Eisenhower was named to the <a href="/wiki/World_Golf_Hall_of_Fame" title="World Golf Hall of Fame">World Golf Hall of Fame</a> in the Lifetime Achievement category for his contributions to the sport.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253"><span>[</span>253<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Eisenhower's name was given to a variety of streets, avenues, etc., in cities around the world, including Paris, France.</li>
</ul>

<p>Note - Eisenhower relinquished his active duty status when he became president on January 20, 1953. He was returned to active duty when he left office eight years later.</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Kay_Summersby" title="Kay Summersby">Kay Summersby</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ike:_Countdown_to_D-Day" title="Ike: Countdown to D-Day">Ike: Countdown to D-Day</a></i>&#160;– a 2004 American television film about the decisions Eisenhower made as Supreme Commander that led to the successful D-Day invasion of World War II</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pressure_(play)" title="Pressure (play)">Pressure</a></i>&#160;– a 2014 British play on Eisenhower's part in the meteorological decisions leading up to D-Day; he was played in the premiere production by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Sinclair" title="Malcolm Sinclair">Malcolm Sinclair</a></li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Eisenhower lived in 'Telegraph Cottage', Warren Road, <a href="/wiki/Coombe,_Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Coombe, Kingston upon Thames">Coombe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Kingston upon Thames">Kingston upon Thames</a> from 1942 to 1944. In 1995, a plaque commemorating this was placed there by the Royal Borough of Kingston upon Thames. It can be seen at the north end of Warren Road.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-210">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Leviero, Anthony (June 9, 1956). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://search.proquest.com.dclibrary.idm.oclc.org/docview/113808030/74C2B9F0012B4528PQ/7?accountid=46320">"President Undergoes Surgery on Intestine Block at 2:59 A.M.: Doctors Pronounce It Success&#160;: Condition Is Good: Operation Lasts Hour and 53 Minutes–13 Attend Him"</a>. <i>The New York Times</i>. p.&#160;1. <q>"President Eisenhower was operated on at 2:59 A.M. today for relief of an intestinal obstruction. At 4:55 A.M., the operation was pronounced a success by the surgeons. ... The President's condition was diagnosed as ileitis. This is an inflamation of the ileum—the lowest portion of the small intestine, where it joins the large intestine. ... The President first felt ill shortly after midnight yesterday. He had attended a dinner of the White House News Photographers Association Thursday night and had returned to the White House at 11. Mrs. Eisenhower called Maj. Gen. Howard McC. Snyder, the President's personal physician, at 12:45 A.M. yesterday, telling him the President had some discomfort in his stomach. He recommended a slight dose of milk of magnesia. At 1:20 Mrs. Eisenhower called again, saying the President was still complaining of not feeling well. This time she asked Dr. Snyder to come to the White House from his home about a mile away on Connecticut Avenue. He arrived at 2 A.M. and has not left the President's side since.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADwight+D.+Eisenhower&amp;rft.atitle=President+Undergoes+Surgery+on+Intestine+Block+at+2%3A59+A.M.%3A+Doctors+Pronounce+It+Success+%3A+Condition+Is+Good%3A+Operation+Lasts+Hour+and+53+Minutes%E2%80%9313+Attend+Him&amp;rft.aufirst=Anthony&amp;rft.aulast=Leviero&amp;rft.date=June+9%2C+1956&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fsearch.proquest.com.dclibrary.idm.oclc.org%2Fdocview%2F113808030%2F74C2B9F0012B4528PQ%2F7%3Faccountid%3D46320&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.pages=1&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li>Harris, Seymour E. (1962). <i>The Economics of the Political Parties, with Special Attention to Presidents Eisenhower and Kennedy</i>.</li>
<li>Medhurst, Martin J. (1993). <i>Dwight D. Eisenhower: Strategic Communicator</i> Greenwood Press.</li>
<li>Mayer, Michael S. (2009). <i>The Eisenhower Years</i>, 1024 pp; short biographies by experts of 500 prominent figures, with some primary sources.</li>
<li>Newton, Jim. (2011) <i>Eisenhower: The White House Years</i></li>
<li>Pach, Chester J. and Richardson, Elmo (1991). <i>Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower</i>. Standard scholarly survey.</li>
<li>Prados, John. (2015) "Eisenhower and the Cold War Arms Race: 'Open Skies' and the Military-Industrial Complex." <i>Journal of Cold War Studies</i>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography_and_interpretations_by_scholars">Historiography and interpretations by scholars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwight_D._Eisenhower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Historiography and interpretations by scholars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Burk, Robert. "Eisenhower Revisionism Revisited: Reflections on Eisenhower Scholarship", <i>Historian</i>, Spring 1988, Vol. 50, Issue 2, pp.&#160;196–209</li>
<li>McAuliffe, Mary S. "Eisenhower, the President", <i>Journal of American History</i> 68 (1981), pp.&#160;625–632 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/pss/1901942">in JSTOR</a></li>
<li>McMahon, Robert J. "Eisenhower and Third World Nationalism: A Critique of the Revisionists," <i>Political Science Quarterly</i> (1986) 101#3 pp.&#160;453–473 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/2151625">in JSTOR</a></li>
<li>Polsky, Andrew J. "Shifting Currents: Dwight Eisenhower and the Dynamic of Presidential Opportunity Structure," <i>Presidential Studies Quarterly</i>, March 2015.</li>
<li>Rabe, Stephen G. "Eisenhower Revisionism: A Decade of Scholarship," <i>Diplomatic History</i> (1993) 17#1 pp&#160;97–115.</li>
<li>Schlesinger Jr., Arthur. "The Ike Age Revisited," <i>Reviews in American History</i> (1983) 11#1 pp.&#160;1–11 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/2701865">in JSTOR</a></li>
<li>Streeter, Stephen M. "Interpreting the 1954 U.S. Intervention In Guatemala: Realist, Revisionist, and Postrevisionist Perspectives," <i>History Teacher</i> (2000) 34#1 pp&#160;61–74. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/305437">in JSTOR</a></li>
<li>Watry, David M. <i>Diplomacy at the Brink: Eisenhower, Churchill, and Eden in the Cold War.</i> Baton Rouge: Louisiana State University Press, 2014.</li>
</ul>
